href="/wiki/File:E%3Dmc%C2%B2-explication.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="324" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/E%3Dmc%C2%B2-explication.svg/236px-E%3Dmc%C2%B2-explication.svg.png" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/E%3Dmc%C2%B2-explication.svg/472px-E%3Dmc%C2%B2-explication.svg.png 2x" width="236"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:E%3Dmc%C2%B2-explication.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><span class="texhtml"><i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup><small>2</small></sup></span> explained</div></div></div>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, <b>mass&#8211;energy equivalence</b> states that anything having <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> has an equivalent amount of <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> and vice versa, with these fundamental quantities directly relating to one another by <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>\'s famous formula:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-famous_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-famous-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p><p><div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E=mc^{2}}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E=mc^{2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></div><img alt="{\\displaystyle E=mc^{2}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9f73dbd37a0cac34406ee89057fa1b36a1e6a18e" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:8.976ex; height:2.676ex;"/></div>\n</p><p>This formula states that the equivalent energy (<span class="texhtml"><i>E</i></span>) can be calculated as the mass (<span class="texhtml"><i>m</i></span>) multiplied by the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a> (<span class="texhtml"><i>c</i></span> = about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7008300000000000000&#9824;</span>3<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>8</sup></span> m/s) squared. Similarly, anything having energy exhibits a corresponding mass <span class="texhtml"><i>m</i></span> given by its energy <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i></span> divided by the speed of light squared <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i>&#178;</span>. Because the speed of light is a very large number in everyday units, the formula implies that even an everyday object at rest with a modest amount of mass has a very large amount of energy intrinsically. <a href="/wiki/Chemical_reaction" title="Chemical reaction">Chemical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reaction" title="Nuclear reaction">nuclear</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Energy_transformation" title="Energy transformation">energy transformations</a> may cause a <a href="/wiki/Physical_system" title="Physical system">system</a> to lose some of its energy content (and thus some corresponding mass), releasing it as the <a href="/wiki/Radiant_energy" title="Radiant energy">radiant energy</a> of <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a> or as <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal</a> energy for example.\n</p><p>Mass&#8211;energy equivalence arose originally from <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special relativity</a> as a paradox described by <a href="/wiki/Henri_Poincar%C3%A9" title="Henri Poincar&#233;">Henri Poincar&#233;</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-action_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-action-2">[2]</a></sup> Einstein proposed it on 21 November 1905, in the paper <i>Does the inertia of a body depend upon its energy-content?</i>, one of his <i><a href="/wiki/Annus_Mirabilis_papers" title="Annus Mirabilis papers">Annus Mirabilis (Miraculous Year) papers</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inertia_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-inertia-3">[3]</a></sup> Einstein was the first to propose that the equivalence of mass and energy is a general principle and a consequence of the <a href="/wiki/Spacetime_symmetries" title="Spacetime symmetries">symmetries of space and time</a>.\n</p><p>A consequence of the mass&#8211;energy equivalence is that if a body is stationary, it still has some internal or intrinsic energy, called its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intrinsic_mass#Rest_energy" title="Intrinsic mass">rest energy</a>, corresponding to its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rest_mass" title="Rest mass">rest mass</a>.  When the body is in motion, its total energy is greater than its rest energy, and equivalently its <i>total mass</i> (also called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relativistic_mass" title="Relativistic mass">relativistic mass</a> in this context) is greater than its rest mass.  This rest mass is also called the intrinsic or <a href="/wiki/Invariant_mass" title="Invariant mass">invariant mass</a> because it remains the same regardless of this motion, even for the extreme speeds or gravity considered in special and <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a>.\n</p><p>The mass&#8211;energy formula also serves to <a href="/wiki/Conversion_of_units" title="Conversion of units">convert</a> <a href="/wiki/Category:Units_of_mass" title="Category:Units of mass">units of mass</a> to <a href="/wiki/Category:Units_of_energy" title="Category:Units of energy">units of energy</a> (and vice versa), no matter what <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Systems_of_measurement" title="Systems of measurement">system of measurement units</a> is used.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 gravity</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-10"><a href="#Application_to_nuclear_physics"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Application to nuclear class="tocnumber">8.2</span> <span class="toctext">Relativistic mass</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Low_speed_expansion"><span class="tocnumber">8.3</span> <span class="toctext">Low speed expansion</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Newton:_matter_and_light"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Newton: matter and light</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href=\'#Swedenborg:_matter_composed_of_"pure_and_total_motion"\'><span class="tocnumber">9.2</span> <span class="toctext">Swedenborg: matter composed of "pure and total motion"</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Electromagnetic_mass"><span class="tocnumber">9.3</span> <span class="toctext">Electromagnetic mass</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Radiation_pressure_and_inertia"><span class="tocnumber">9.4</span> <span class="toctext">Radiation pressure and inertia</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Einstein:_mass&#8211;energy_equivalence"><span class="tocnumber">9.5</span> <span class="toctext">Einstein: mass&#8211;energy equivalence</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-23"><a href="#The_first_derivation_by_Einstein_(1905)"><span class="tocnumber">9.5.1</span> <span class="toctext">The first derivation by Einstein (1905)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-24"><a href="#Alternative_version"><span class="tocnumber">9.5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Alternative version</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-25"><a href="#Relativistic_center-of-mass_theorem_(1906)"><span class="tocnumber">9.5.3</span> <span class="toctext">Relativistic center-of-mass theorem (1906)</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Others"><span class="tocnumber">9.6</span> <span class="toctext">Others</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Radioactivity_and_nuclear_energy"><span class="tocnumber">9.7</span> <span class="toctext">Radioactivity and nuclear energy</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature">Nomenclature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Nomenclature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The formula was initially written in many different notations, and its interpretation and justification was further developed in several steps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jammer2_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-jammer2-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hecht_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-hecht-5">[5]</a></sup>\nIn "Does the inertia of a body depend upon its energy content?" (1905), Einstein used <span class="texhtml"><i>V</i></span> to mean the speed of light in a vacuum and <span class="texhtml"><i>L</i></span> to mean the <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> lost by a body in the form of <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inertia_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-inertia-3">[3]</a></sup> Consequently, the equation <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup></span> was not originally written as a formula but as a sentence in German saying that "if a body gives off the energy <i><span class="texhtml"></span></i>L<i></i> in the form of radiation, its mass diminishes by <span class="texhtml"><span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;"><i>L</i></span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;"><i>V</i><sup>2</sup></span></span></span>." A remark placed above it informed that the equation was approximated by neglecting "magnitudes of fourth and higher orders" of a <a href="/wiki/Series_(mathematics)" title="Series (mathematics)">series expansion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In May 1907, Einstein explained that the expression for energy <span class="texhtml"><i>&#949;</i></span> of a moving mass point assumes the simplest form, when its expression for the state of rest is chosen to be <span class="texhtml"><i>&#949;</i><sub>0</sub> = <i>&#956;V</i><sup>2</sup></span> (where <span class="texhtml"><i>&#956;</i></span> is the mass), which is in agreement with the "principle of the equivalence of mass and energy". In addition, Einstein used the formula <span class="texhtml"><i>&#956;</i> = <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;"><i>E</i><sub>0</sub></span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;"><i>V</i><sup>2</sup></span></span></span>, with <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i><sub>0</sub></span> being the energy of a system of mass points, to describe the energy and mass increase of that system when the velocity of the differently moving mass points is increased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In June 1907, <a href="/wiki/Max_Planck" title="Max Planck">Max Planck</a> rewrote Einstein\'s mass&#8211;energy relationship as <span class="texhtml"><i>M</i> = <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;"><i>E</i><sub>0</sub> + <i>pV</i><sub>0</sub></span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;"><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span></span></span>, where <span class="texhtml"><i>p</i></span> is the pressure and <span class="texhtml"><i>V</i></span> the volume to express the relation between mass, its <i>latent energy</i>, and thermodynamic energy within the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Subsequently, in October 1907, this was rewritten as <span class="texhtml"><i>M</i><sub>0</sub> = <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;"><i>E</i><sub>0</sub></span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;"><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span></span></span> and given a quantum interpretation by <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Stark" title="Johannes Stark">Johannes Stark</a>, who assumed its validity and correctness (<i>G&#252;ltigkeit</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In December 1907, Einstein expressed the equivalence in the form <span class="texhtml"><i>M</i> = <i>&#956;</i> + <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;"><i>E</i><sub>0</sub></span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;"><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span></span></span> and concluded: "A mass <i><span class="texhtml"></span></i>&#956;<i></i> is equivalent, as regards inertia, to a quantity of energy <i><span class="texhtml"></span></i>&#956;c<i><sup>2</sup></i>. [...] It appears far more natural to consider every inertial mass as a store of energy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1909, <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_N._Lewis" title="Gilbert N. Lewis">Gilbert N. Lewis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_C._Tolman" title="Richard C. Tolman">Richard C. Tolman</a> used two variations of the formula: <span class="texhtml"><i>m</i> = <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;"><i>E</i></span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;"><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span></span></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>m</i><sub>0</sub> = <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;"><i>E</i><sub>0</sub></span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;"><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span></span></span>, with <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">E</span> being the relativistic energy (the energy of an object when the object is moving), <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i><sub>0</sub></span> is the rest energy (the energy when not moving), <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">m</span> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relativistic_mass" title="Relativistic mass">relativistic mass</a> (the rest mass and the extra mass gained when moving), and <span class="texhtml"><i>m</i><sub>0</sub></span> is the <a href="/wiki/Invariant_mass" title="Invariant mass">rest mass</a> (the mass when not moving).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The same relations in different notation were used by <a href="/wiki/Hendrik_Lorentz" title="Hendrik Lorentz">Hendrik Lorentz</a> in 1913 (published 1914), though he placed the energy on the left-hand side: <span class="texhtml"><i>&#949;</i> = <i>Mc</i><sup>2</sup></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>&#949;</i><sub>0</sub> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup></span>, with <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">&#949;</span> being the total energy (rest energy plus kinetic energy) of a moving material point, <span class="texhtml"><i>&#949;</i><sub>0</sub></span> its rest energy, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">M</span> the relativistic mass, and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">m</span> the invariant (or rest) mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1911, <a href="/wiki/Max_von_Laue" title="Max von Laue">Max von Laue</a> gave a more comprehensive proof of <span class="texhtml"><i>M</i><sub>0</sub> = <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;"><i>E</i><sub>0</sub></span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;"><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span></span></span> from the <a href="/wiki/Stress%E2%80%93energy_tensor" title="Stress&#8211;energy tensor">stress&#8211;energy tensor</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> which was later (1918) generalized by <a href="/wiki/Felix_Klein" title="Felix Klein">Felix Klein</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Einstein returned to the topic once again after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> and this time he wrote <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup></span> in the title of his article<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> intended as an explanation for a general reader by analogy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation_of_mass_and_energy">Conservation of mass and energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Conservation of mass and energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Conservation_of_energy" title="Conservation of energy">Conservation of energy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Conservation_of_mass" title="Conservation of mass">Conservation of mass</a></div>\n<p>Mass and energy can be seen as two names (and two measurement units) for the same underlying, conserved physical quantity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Thus, the laws of <a href="/wiki/Conservation_of_energy" title="Conservation of energy">conservation of energy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mass_in_special_relativity" title="Mass in special relativity">conservation of (total) mass</a> are equivalent and both hold true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Einstein elaborated in a 1946 essay that "the principle of the conservation of mass [...] proved inadequate in the face of the special theory of relativity.  It was therefore merged with the energy <a href="/wiki/Conservation_law" title="Conservation law">conservation</a> principle&#8212;just as, about 60 years before, the principle of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_of_mechanical_energy" title="Conservation of mechanical energy">conservation of mechanical energy</a> had been combined with the principle of the conservation of heat [thermal energy].  We might say that the principle of the conservation of energy, having previously swallowed up that of the conservation of heat, now proceeded to swallow that of the conservation of mass&#8212;and holds the field alone."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p><p>If the conservation of mass law is interpreted as conservation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rest_mass" title="Rest mass"><i>rest</i> mass</a>, it does not hold true in special relativity.  The <i>rest</i> energy (equivalently, rest mass) of a particle can be converted, not "to energy" (it already <i>is</i> energy (mass)), but rather to <i>other</i> forms of energy (mass) that require motion, such as <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Radiant_energy" title="Radiant energy">radiant energy</a>. Similarly, kinetic or radiant energy can be converted to other kinds of particles that have rest energy (rest mass).  In the transformation process, neither the total amount of mass nor the total amount of energy changes, since both properties are connected via a simple constant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A._Wheeler,_1992._pp._248_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-A._Wheeler,_1992._pp._248-22">[22]</a></sup> This view requires that if either energy or (total) mass disappears from a system, it is always found that both have simply moved to another place, where they are both measurable as an increase of both energy and mass that corresponds to the loss in the first system.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fast-moving_objects_and_systems_of_objects">Fast-moving objects and systems of objects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Fast-moving objects and systems of objects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>When an object is pushed in the direction of motion, it gains <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a> and energy, but when the object is already traveling near the speed of light, it cannot move much faster, no matter how much energy it absorbs. Its momentum and energy continue to increase without bounds, whereas its speed approaches (but never reaches) a constant value&#8212;the speed of light. This implies that in relativity the momentum of an object cannot be a constant times the <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocity</a>, nor can the <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy#Kinetic_energy_of_rigid_bodies" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> be a constant times the square of the velocity.\n</p><p>A property called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relativistic_mass" title="Relativistic mass">relativistic mass</a> is defined as the ratio of the momentum of an object to its velocity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Relativistic mass depends on the motion of the object, so that different observers in relative motion see different values for it. If the object is moving slowly, the relativistic mass is nearly equal to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rest_mass" title="Rest mass">rest mass</a> and both are nearly equal to the usual Newtonian mass. If the object is moving quickly, the relativistic mass is greater than the rest mass by an amount equal to the mass associated with the <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> of the object. As the object approaches the speed of light, the relativistic mass grows infinitely, because the kinetic energy grows infinitely and this energy is associated with mass.\n</p><p>The relativistic mass is always equal to the total energy (rest energy plus kinetic energy) divided by <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tipler_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-tipler-24">[24]</a></sup> Because the relativistic mass is exactly proportional to the energy, relativistic mass and relativistic energy are nearly synonyms; the only difference between them is the <a href="/wiki/Unit_of_measurement" title="Unit of measurement">units</a>. If length and time are measured in <a href="/wiki/Natural_units" title="Natural units">natural units</a>, the speed of light is equal to 1, and even this difference disappears. Then mass and energy have the same units and are always equal, so it is redundant to speak about relativistic mass, because it is just another name for the energy. This is why physicists usually reserve the useful short word "mass" to mean rest mass, or <a href="/wiki/Invariant_mass" title="Invariant mass">invariant mass</a>, and not relativistic mass.\n</p><p>The relativistic mass of a moving object is larger than the relativistic mass of an object that is not moving, because a moving object has extra kinetic energy. The <i>rest mass</i> of an object is defined as the mass of an object when it is at rest, so that the rest mass is always the same, independent of the motion of the observer: it is the same in all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inertial_frame" title="Inertial frame">inertial frames</a>.\n</p><p>For things and systems made up of many parts, like an <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">atomic nucleus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">star</a>, the relativistic mass is the sum of the relativistic masses (or energies) of the parts, because energies are additive in isolated systems. This is not true in open systems, however, if energy is subtracted. For example, if a system is <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy#Mass-energy_relation" title="Binding energy"><i>bound</i></a> by attractive forces, and the energy gained due to the forces of attraction in excess of the work done is removed from the system, then mass is lost with this removed energy. For example, the mass of an atomic nucleus is less than the total mass of the protons and neutrons that make it up, but this is only true after this energy from binding has been removed in the form of a gamma ray (which in this system, carries away the mass of the energy of binding). This mass decrease is also equivalent to the energy required to break up the nucleus into individual protons and neutrons (in this case, work and mass would need to be supplied). Similarly, the mass of the solar system is slightly less than the sum of the individual masses of the sun and planets.\n</p><p>For a system of particles going off in different directions, the <a href="/wiki/Invariant_mass" title="Invariant mass">invariant mass</a> of the system is the analog of the rest mass, and is the same for all observers, even those in relative motion. It is defined as the total energy (divided by <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span>) in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Center_of_mass_frame" title="Center of mass frame">center of mass frame</a> (where by definition, the system total momentum is zero). A simple example of an object with moving parts but zero total momentum is a container of gas. In this case, the mass of the container is given by its total energy (including the kinetic energy of the gas molecules), since the system total energy and invariant mass are the same in any reference frame where the momentum is zero, and such a reference frame is also the only frame in which the object can be weighed. In a similar way, the theory of special relativity posits that the thermal energy in all objects (including solids) contributes to their total masses and weights, even though this energy is present as the kinetic and potential energies of the atoms in the object, and it (in a similar way to the gas) is not seen in the rest masses of the atoms that make up the object.\n</p><p>In a similar manner, even photons (light quanta), if trapped in a container space (as a <a href="/wiki/Photon_gas" title="Photon gas">photon gas</a> or <a href="/wiki/Thermal_radiation" title="Thermal radiation">thermal radiation</a>), would contribute a mass associated with their energy to the container. Such an extra mass, in theory, could be weighed in the same way as any other type of rest mass. This is true in special relativity theory, even though individually photons have no rest mass. The property that trapped energy <i>in any form</i> adds weighable mass to systems that have no net momentum is one of the characteristic and notable consequences of relativity. It has no counterpart in classical Newtonian physics, in which radiation, light, heat, and kinetic energy never exhibit weighable mass under any circumstances.\n</p><p>Just as the relativistic mass of an isolated system is conserved through time, so also is its invariant mass.This property allows the conservation of all types of mass in systems, and also conservation of all types of mass in reactions where matter is destroyed (annihilated), leaving behind the energy that was associated with it (which is now in non-material form, rather than material form). Matter may appear and disappear in various reactions, but mass and energy are both unchanged in this process.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applicability_of_the_strict_formula">Applicability of the strict formula</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Applicability of the strict formula">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>As is noted above, two different definitions of mass have been used in special relativity, and also two different definitions of energy. The simple equation <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E=mc^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E=mc^{2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E = mc^2" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9f73dbd37a0cac34406ee89057fa1b36a1e6a18e" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:8.976ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span> is not generally applicable to all these types of mass and energy, except in the special  case that the total additive momentum is zero for the system under consideration. In such a case, which is always guaranteed when observing the system from either its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Center_of_mass_frame" title="Center of mass frame">center of mass frame</a> or its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Center_of_momentum_frame" title="Center of momentum frame">center of momentum frame</a>, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E=mc^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E=mc^{2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E = mc^2" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9f73dbd37a0cac34406ee89057fa1b36a1e6a18e" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:8.976ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span> is always true for any type of mass and energy that are chosen. Thus, for example, in the center of mass frame, the total energy of an object or system is equal to its rest mass times <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle c^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle c^{2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="c^{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f3386a00382ce857fb0b3b04b9fa2bbe5cfae9" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.061ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span>, a useful equality. This is the relationship used for the container of gas in the previous example. It is <i>not</i> true in other reference frames where the center of mass is in motion. In these systems or for such an object, its total energy depends on both its rest (or invariant) mass, and its (total) momentum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In inertial reference frames other than the rest frame or center of mass frame, the equation <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E=mc^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E=mc^{2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E = mc^2" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9f73dbd37a0cac34406ee89057fa1b36a1e6a18e" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:8.976ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span> remains true if the energy is the relativistic energy <i>and</i> the mass is the relativistic mass. It is also correct if the energy is the rest or invariant energy (also the minimum energy), <i>and</i> the mass is the rest mass, or the invariant mass. However, connection of the <b>total or relativistic energy</b> (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E_{r}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>r</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E_{r}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E_{r}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/293328c22d5276d48118b81fdd39033c93c100e1" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.689ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>) with the <b>rest or invariant mass</b> (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle m_{0}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle m_{0}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="m_{0}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3a6ff51ee949104fe6fae553cfbdfba29d5fac1e" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:3.095ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span>) requires consideration of the system\'s total momentum, in systems and reference frames where the total momentum (of <a href="/wiki/Magnitude_(mathematics)#Euclidean_vectors" title="Magnitude (mathematics)">magnitude</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>p</i></span>) has a non-zero value. The formula then required to connect the two different kinds of mass and energy, is the extended version of Einstein\'s equation, called the relativistic <a href="/wiki/Energy%E2%80%93momentum_relation" title="Energy&#8211;momentum relation">energy&#8211;momentum relation</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\begin{aligned}E_{r}^{2}-|{\\vec {p}}\\,|^{2}c^{2}&amp;=m_{0}^{2}c^{4}\\\\E_{r}^{2}-(pc)^{2}&amp;=(m_{0}c^{2})^{2}\\end{aligned}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mtable columnalign="right left right left right left right left right left right left" columnspacing="0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em" displaystyle="true" rowspacing="3pt">\n<mtr>\n<mtd>\n<msubsup>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>r</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msubsup>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n</mover>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>\n<msup>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mtd>\n<mtd>\n<mi></mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msubsup>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msubsup>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>4</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mtd>\n</mtr>\n<mtr>\n<mtd>\n<msubsup>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>r</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msubsup>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>p</mi>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<msup>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mtd>\n<mtd>\n<mi></mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<msup>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mtd>\n</mtr>\n</mtable>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\begin{aligned}E_{r}^{2}-|{\\vec {p}}\\,|^{2}c^{2}&amp;=m_{0}^{2}c^{4}\\\\E_{r}^{2}-(pc)^{2}&amp;=(m_{0}c^{2})^{2}\\end{aligned}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\begin{aligned}E_{r}^{2}-|{\\vec {p}}\\,|^{2}c^{2}&amp;=m_{0}^{2}c^{4}\\\\E_{r}^{2}-(pc)^{2}&amp;=(m_{0}c^{2})^{2}\\end{aligned}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/52661b4a42f2a714bbadd5c1d2e629e0762fb46d" style="vertical-align: -2.838ex; width:23.679ex; height:6.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>or\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E_{r}={\\sqrt {(m_{0}c^{2})^{2}+(pc)^{2}}}\\,\\!}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>r</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msqrt>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<msup>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>p</mi>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<msup>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</msqrt>\n</mrow>\n<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>\n<mspace width="negativethinmathspace"></mspace>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E_{r}={\\sqrt {(m_{0}c^{2})^{2}+(pc)^{2}}}\\,\\!}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E_{r}={\\sqrt {(m_{0}c^{2})^{2}+(pc)^{2}}}\\,\\!" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8b5f9843c419229d0e3ac5075c1463d4851cd5cd" style="vertical-align: -1.671ex; margin-right: -0.387ex; width:24.398ex; height:4.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>Here the <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle (pc)^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>p</mi>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<msup>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle (pc)^{2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle (pc)^{2}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2a43d9dbb233ba82385c2ab8c0e53001f6215df7" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:5.04ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span> term represents the square of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euclidean_norm" title="Euclidean norm">Euclidean norm</a> (total vector length) of the various momentum vectors in the system, which reduces to the square of the simple momentum magnitude, if only a single particle is considered. This equation reduces to <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E=mc^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E=mc^{2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E = mc^2" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9f73dbd37a0cac34406ee89057fa1b36a1e6a18e" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:8.976ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span> when the momentum term is zero. For photons where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle m_{0}=0}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle m_{0}=0}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle m_{0}=0}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/225a6f46051451ffb75d945be4a60e4a441a8682" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:7.356ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>, the equation reduces to <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E_{r}=pc}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>r</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>p</mi>\n<mi>c</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E_{r}=pc}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle E_{r}=pc}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8b61795965015ca2e933fe4ac8a1f1a0731909e4" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:7.964ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Meanings_of_the_strict_formula">Meanings of the strict formula</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Meanings of the strict formula">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="box-More_citations_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br/><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Mass%E2%80%93energy+equivalence%22" rel="nofollow">"Mass&#8211;energy equivalence"</a>&#160;&#8211;&#160;<a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Mass%E2%80%93energy+equivalence%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">news</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Mass%E2%80%93energy+equivalence%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers" rel="nofollow">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Mass%E2%80%93energy+equivalence%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">books</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Mass%E2%80%93energy+equivalence%22" rel="nofollow">scholar</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Mass%E2%80%93energy+equivalence%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on" rel="nofollow">JSTOR</a></span></small></span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">February 2016</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:E_equals_m_plus_c_square_at_Taipei101.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/E_equals_m_plus_c_square_at_Taipei101.jpg/220px-E_equals_m_plus_c_square_at_Taipei101.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/62/E_equals_m_plus_c_square_at_Taipei101.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:E_equals_m_plus_c_square_at_Taipei101.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The mass&#8211;energy equivalence formula was displayed on <a href="/wiki/Taipei_101" title="Taipei 101">Taipei 101</a> during the event of the <a href="/wiki/World_Year_of_Physics_2005" title="World Year of Physics 2005">World Year of Physics 2005</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Mass&#8211;energy equivalence states that any object has a certain energy, even when it is stationary. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newtonian_mechanics" title="Newtonian mechanics">Newtonian mechanics</a>, a motionless body has no <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a>, and it may or may not have other amounts of internal stored energy, like <a href="/wiki/Chemical_energy" title="Chemical energy">chemical energy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a>, in addition to any <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">potential energy</a> it may have from its position in a <a href="/wiki/Field_(physics)" title="Field (physics)">field of force</a>. In Newtonian mechanics, all of these energies are much smaller than the mass of the object times the speed of light squared.\n</p><p>In relativity, all the energy that moves with an object (that is, all the energy present in the object\'s rest frame) contributes to the total mass of the body, which measures how much it resists acceleration. Each bit of potential and kinetic energy makes a proportional contribution to the mass. As noted above, even if a box of ideal mirrors "contains" light, then the individually massless photons still contribute to the total mass of the box, by the amount of their energy divided by <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_relativity" title="Theory of relativity">relativity</a>, removing energy is removing mass, and for an observer in the center of mass frame, the formula <span class="texhtml"><i>m</i> = <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;"><i>E</i></span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;"><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span></span></span> indicates how much mass is lost when energy is removed. In a nuclear reaction, the mass of the atoms that come out is less than the mass of the atoms that go in, and the difference in mass shows up as heat and light with the same relativistic mass as the difference (and also the same <a href="/wiki/Invariant_mass" title="Invariant mass">invariant mass</a> in the center of mass frame of the system). In this case, the <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">E</span> in the formula is the energy released and removed, and the mass <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">m</span> is how much the mass decreases. In the same way, when any sort of energy is added to an isolated system, the increase in the mass is equal to the added energy divided by <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span>. For example, when water is heated it gains about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6983111000000000000&#9824;</span>1.11<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>&#8722;17</sup>&#160;kg</span> of mass for every joule of heat added to the water.\n</p><p>An object moves with different speed in different frames, depending on the motion of the observer, so the kinetic energy in both Newtonian mechanics and relativity is <i>frame dependent</i>. This means that the amount of relativistic energy, and therefore the amount of relativistic mass, that an object is measured to have depends on the observer. The <i>rest mass</i> is defined as the mass that an object has when it is not moving (or when an inertial frame is chosen such that it is not moving). The term also applies to the invariant mass of systems when the system as a whole is not "moving" (has no net momentum). The rest and invariant masses are the smallest possible value of the mass of the object or system. They also are conserved quantities, so long as the system is isolated. Because of the way they are calculated, the effects of moving observers are subtracted, so these quantities do not change with the motion of the observer.\n</p><p>The rest mass is almost never additive: the rest mass of an object is not the sum of the rest masses of its parts. The rest mass of an object is the total energy of all the parts, including kinetic energy, as measured by an observer that sees the center of the mass of the object to be standing still. The rest mass adds up only if the parts are standing still and do not attract or repel, so that they do not have any extra kinetic or potential energy. The other possibility is that they have a positive kinetic energy and a negative potential energy that exactly cancels.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Binding_energy_and_the_.22mass_defect.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id=\'Binding_energy_and_the_"mass_defect"\'>Binding energy and the "mass defect"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title=\'Edit section: Binding energy and the "mass defect"\'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">Binding energy</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_defect" title="Mass defect">Mass defect</a></div>\n<table class="box-Refimprove_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br/><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Mass%E2%80%93energy+equivalence%22" rel="nofollow">"Mass&#8211;energy equivalence"</a>&#160;&#8211;&#160;<a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Mass%E2%80%93energy+equivalence%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">news</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Mass%E2%80%93energy+equivalence%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers" rel="nofollow">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Mass%E2%80%93energy+equivalence%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">books</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Mass%E2%80%93energy+equivalence%22" rel="nofollow">scholar</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Mass%E2%80%93energy+equivalence%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on" rel="nofollow">JSTOR</a></span></small></span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">July 2013</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Whenever any type of energy is removed from a system, the mass associated with the energy is also removed, and the system therefore loses mass. This mass defect in the system may be simply calculated as <span class="texhtml">&#916;<i>m</i> = <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">&#916;<i>E</i></span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;"><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span></span></span>, and this was the form of the equation historically first presented by Einstein in 1905. However, use of this formula in such circumstances has led to the false idea that mass has been "converted" to energy. This may be particularly the case when the energy (and mass) removed from the system is associated with the <i>binding energy</i> of the system. In such cases, the binding energy is observed as a "mass defect" or deficit in the new system.\n</p><p>The fact that the released energy is not easily weighed in many such cases, may cause its mass to be neglected as though it no longer existed. This circumstance has encouraged the false idea of conversion of <i>mass</i> to energy, rather than the correct idea that the binding energy of such systems is relatively large, and exhibits a measurable mass, which is removed when the binding energy is removed.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.\n</p><p>The difference between the rest mass of a bound system and of the unbound parts is the <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">binding energy</a> of the system, if this energy has been removed after binding. For example, a water molecule weighs a little less than two free hydrogen atoms and an oxygen atom. The minuscule mass difference is the energy needed to split the molecule into three individual atoms (divided by <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span>), which was given off as heat when the molecule formed (this heat had mass). Likewise, a stick of dynamite in theory weighs a little bit more than the fragments after the explosion, but this is true only so long as the fragments are cooled and the heat removed. In this case the mass difference is the energy/heat that is released when the dynamite explodes, and when this heat escapes, the mass associated with it escapes, only to be deposited in the surroundings, which absorb the heat (so that total mass is conserved).\n</p><p>Such a change in mass may only happen when the system is open, and the energy and mass escapes. Thus, if a stick of dynamite is blown up in a hermetically sealed chamber, the mass of the chamber and fragments, the heat, sound, and light would still be equal to the original mass of the chamber and dynamite. If sitting on a scale, the weight and mass would not change. This would in theory also happen even with a nuclear bomb, if it could be kept in an ideal box of infinite strength, which did not rupture or pass radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A._Wheeler,_1992._pp._248_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-A._Wheeler,_1992._pp._248-22">[22]</a></sup> Thus, a 21.5&#160;<a href="/wiki/TNT_equivalent" title="TNT equivalent">kiloton</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7013900000000000000&#9824;</span>9<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>13</sup>&#160;joule</span>) nuclear bomb produces about one gram of heat and electromagnetic radiation, but the mass of this energy would not be detectable in an exploded bomb in an ideal box sitting on a scale; instead, the contents of the box would be heated to millions of degrees without changing total mass and weight. If then, however, a transparent window (passing only electromagnetic radiation) were opened in such an ideal box after the explosion, and a beam of X-rays and other lower-energy light allowed to escape the box, it would eventually be found to weigh one gram less than it had before the explosion. This weight loss and mass loss would happen as the box was cooled by this process, to room temperature. However, any surrounding mass that absorbed the X-rays (and other "heat") would <i>gain</i> this gram of mass from the resulting heating, so the mass "loss" would represent merely its relocation. Thus, no mass (or, in the case of a nuclear bomb, no matter) would be "converted" to energy in such a process. Mass and energy, as always, would both be separately conserved.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Massless_particles">Massless particles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Massless particles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Massless particles have zero rest mass. Their relativistic mass is simply their relativistic energy, divided by <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span>, or <span class="texhtml"><i>m</i><sub><small>rel</small></sub> = <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;"><i>E</i></span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;"><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The energy for photons is <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i> = <i>hf</i></span>, where <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">h</span> is <a href="/wiki/Planck_constant" title="Planck constant">Planck\'s constant</a> and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">f</span> is the photon frequency. This frequency and thus the relativistic energy are frame-dependent.\n</p><p>If an observer runs away from a photon in the direction the photon travels from a source, and it catches up with the observer&#8212;when the photon catches up, the observer sees it as having less energy than it had at the source. The faster the observer is traveling with regard to the source when the photon catches up, the less energy the photon has. As an observer approaches the speed of light with regard to the source, the photon looks redder and redder, by <a href="/wiki/Relativistic_Doppler_effect" title="Relativistic Doppler effect">relativistic Doppler effect</a> (the Doppler shift is the relativistic formula), and the energy of a very long-wavelength photon approaches zero. This is because the photon is <i>massless</i>&#8212;the rest mass of a photon is zero.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Massless_particles_contribute_rest_mass_and_invariant_mass_to_systems">Massless particles contribute rest mass and invariant mass to systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Massless particles contribute rest mass and invariant mass to systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Two photons moving in different directions cannot both be made to have arbitrarily small total energy by changing frames, or by moving toward or away from them. The reason is that in a two-photon system, the energy of one photon is decreased by chasing after it, but the energy of the other increases with the same shift in observer motion. Two photons not moving in the same direction comprise an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inertial_frame" title="Inertial frame">inertial frame</a> where the combined energy is smallest, but not zero. This is called the <a href="/wiki/Center_of_mass" title="Center of mass">center of mass</a> frame or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Center_of_momentum" title="Center of momentum">center of momentum</a> frame; these terms are almost synonyms (the center of mass frame is the special case of a center of momentum frame where the center of mass is put at the origin). The most that chasing a pair of photons can accomplish to decrease their energy is to put the observer in a frame where the photons have equal energy and are moving directly away from each other. In this frame, the observer is now moving in the same direction and speed as the center of mass of the two photons. The total momentum of the photons is now zero, since their momenta are equal and opposite. In this frame the two photons, as a system, have a mass equal to their total energy divided by <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span>. This mass is called the <a href="/wiki/Invariant_mass" title="Invariant mass">invariant mass</a> of the pair of photons together. It is the smallest mass and energy the system may be seen to have, by any observer. It is only the invariant mass of a two-photon system that can be used to make a single particle with the same rest mass.\n</p><p>If the photons are formed by the collision of a particle and an antiparticle, the invariant mass is the same as the total energy of the particle and antiparticle (their rest energy plus the kinetic energy), in the center of mass frame, where they automatically move in equal and opposite directions (since they have equal momentum in this frame). If the photons are formed by the disintegration of a <i>single</i> particle with a well-defined rest mass, like the neutral <a href="/wiki/Pion" title="Pion">pion</a>, the invariant mass of the photons is equal to rest mass of the pion. In this case, the center of mass frame for the pion is just the frame where the pion is at rest, and the center of mass does not change after it disintegrates into two photons. After the two photons are formed, their center of mass is still moving the same way the pion did, and their total energy in this frame adds up to the mass energy of the pion. Thus, by calculating the <a href="/wiki/Invariant_mass" title="Invariant mass">invariant mass</a> of pairs of photons in a particle detector, pairs can be identified that were probably produced by pion disintegration.\n</p><p>A similar calculation illustrates that the invariant mass of systems is conserved, even when massive particles (particles with rest mass) within the system are converted to massless particles (such as photons). In such cases, the photons contribute invariant mass to the system, even though they individually have no invariant mass or rest mass. Thus, an electron and positron (each of which has rest mass) may undergo <a href="/wiki/Electron%E2%80%93positron_annihilation" title="Electron&#8211;positron annihilation">annihilation</a> with each other to produce two photons, each of which is massless (has no rest mass). However, in such circumstances, no system mass is lost. Instead, the system of both photons moving away from each other has an invariant mass, which acts like a rest mass for any system in which the photons are trapped, or that can be weighed. Thus, not only the quantity of relativistic mass, but also the quantity of invariant mass does not change in transformations between "matter" (electrons and positrons) and energy (photons).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relation_to_gravity">Relation to gravity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Relation to gravity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In physics, there are two distinct concepts of <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a>: the gravitational mass and the inertial mass. The gravitational mass is the quantity that determines the strength of the <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_field" title="Gravitational field">gravitational field</a> generated by an object, as well as the gravitational force acting on the object when it is immersed in a gravitational field produced by other bodies. The inertial mass, on the other hand, quantifies how much an object accelerates if a given force is applied to it. The mass&#8211;energy equivalence in special relativity refers to the inertial mass. However, already in the context of Newton gravity, the Weak <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equivalence_Principle" title="Equivalence Principle">Equivalence Principle</a> is postulated: the gravitational and the inertial mass of every object are the same. Thus, the mass&#8211;energy equivalence, combined with the Weak Equivalence Principle, results in the prediction that all forms of energy contribute to the gravitational field generated by an object. This observation is one of the pillars of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_theory_of_relativity" title="General theory of relativity">general theory of relativity</a>.\n</p><p>The above prediction, that all forms of energy interact gravitationally, has been subject to experimental tests. The first observation testing this prediction was made in 1919.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> During a <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_May_29,_1919" title="Solar eclipse of May 29, 1919">solar eclipse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Eddington" title="Arthur Eddington">Arthur Eddington</a> observed that the light from stars passing close to the Sun was bent. The effect is due to the gravitational attraction of light by the Sun. The observation confirmed that the energy carried by light indeed is equivalent to a gravitational mass. Another seminal experiment, the <a href="/wiki/Pound%E2%80%93Rebka_experiment" title="Pound&#8211;Rebka experiment">Pound&#8211;Rebka experiment</a>, was performed in 1960.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> In this test a beam of light was emitted from the top of a tower and detected at the bottom. The frequency of the light detected was higher than the light emitted. This result confirms that the energy of photons increases when they fall in the gravitational field of the Earth. The energy, and therefore the gravitational mass, of photons is proportional to their frequency as stated by the <a href="/wiki/Planck_constant" title="Planck constant">Planck\'s relation</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Application_to_nuclear_physics">Application to nuclear physics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Application to nuclear physics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USS_Enterprise_(CVAN-65),_USS_Long_Beach_(CGN-9)_and_USS_Bainbridge_(DLGN-25)_underway_in_the_Mediterranean_Sea_during_Operation_Sea_Orbit,_in_1964.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="786" data-file-width="1062" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/USS_Enterprise_%28CVAN-65%29%2C_USS_Long_Beach_%28CGN-9%29_and_USS_Bainbridge_%28DLGN-25%29_underway_in_the_Mediterranean_Sea_during_Operation_Sea_Orbit%2C_in_1964.jpg/220px-USS_Enterprise_%28CVAN-65%29%2C_USS_Long_Beach_%28CGN-9%29_and_USS_Bainbridge_%28DLGN-25%29_underway_in_the_Mediterranean_Sea_during_Operation_Sea_Orbit%2C_in_1964.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/USS_Enterprise_%28CVAN-65%29%2C_USS_Long_Beach_%28CGN-9%29_and_USS_Bainbridge_%28DLGN-25%29_underway_in_the_Mediterranean_Sea_during_Operation_Sea_Orbit%2C_in_1964.jpg/330px-USS_Enterprise_%28CVAN-65%29%2C_USS_Long_Beach_%28CGN-9%29_and_USS_Bainbridge_%28DLGN-25%29_underway_in_the_Mediterranean_Sea_during_Operation_Sea_Orbit%2C_in_1964.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/USS_Enterprise_%28CVAN-65%29%2C_USS_Long_Beach_%28CGN-9%29_and_USS_Bainbridge_%28DLGN-25%29_underway_in_the_Mediterranean_Sea_during_Operation_Sea_Orbit%2C_in_1964.jpg/440px-USS_Enterprise_%28CVAN-65%29%2C_USS_Long_Beach_%28CGN-9%29_and_USS_Bainbridge_%28DLGN-25%29_underway_in_the_Mediterranean_Sea_during_Operation_Sea_Orbit%2C_in_1964.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:USS_Enterprise_(CVAN-65),_USS_Long_Beach_(CGN-9)_and_USS_Bainbridge_(DLGN-25)_underway_in_the_Mediterranean_Sea_during_Operation_Sea_Orbit,_in_1964.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Task Force One, the world\'s first nuclear-powered task force. <a href="/wiki/USS_Enterprise_(CVN-65)" title="USS Enterprise (CVN-65)"><i>Enterprise</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/USS_Long_Beach_(CGN-9)" title="USS Long Beach (CGN-9)"><i>Long Beach</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/USS_Bainbridge_(CGN-25)" title="USS Bainbridge (CGN-25)"><i>Bainbridge</i></a> in formation in the Mediterranean, 18 June 1964. <i>Enterprise</i> crew members are spelling out Einstein\'s mass&#8211;energy equivalence formula <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup></span> on the flight deck.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Max_Planck" title="Max Planck">Max Planck</a> pointed out that the mass&#8211;energy equivalence formula implied that bound systems would have a mass less than the sum of their constituents, once the binding energy had been allowed to escape. However, Planck was thinking about chemical reactions, where the binding energy is too small to measure. Einstein suggested that radioactive materials such as <a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">radium</a> would provide a test of the theory, but even though a large amount of energy is released per atom in radium, due to the <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of the substance (1602 years), only a small fraction of radium atoms decay over an experimentally measurable period of time.\n</p><p>Once the nucleus was discovered, experimenters realized that the very high binding energies of the atomic nuclei should allow calculation of their binding energies, simply from mass differences. But it was not until the discovery of the <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a> in 1932, and the measurement of the neutron mass, that this calculation could actually be performed (see <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_binding_energy" title="Nuclear binding energy">nuclear binding energy</a> for example calculation). A little while later, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cockcroft%E2%80%93Walton_accelerator" title="Cockcroft&#8211;Walton accelerator">Cockcroft&#8211;Walton accelerator</a> produced the first <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">transmutation</a> reaction (<span class="nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">7</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>Li + <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">1</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">1</sub></span>p &#8594; 2 <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>He</span>), verifying Einstein\'s formula to an accuracy of &#177;0.5%.\nIn 2005, Rainville et al. published a direct test of the energy-equivalence of mass lost in the binding energy of a neutron to atoms of particular isotopes of silicon and sulfur, by comparing the mass lost to the energy of the emitted gamma ray associated with the neutron capture. The binding mass-loss agreed with the gamma ray energy to a precision of &#177;0.00004%, the most accurate test of <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup></span> to date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rainville_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-rainville-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The mass&#8211;energy equivalence formula was used in the understanding of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a> reactions, and implies the great amount of energy that can be released by a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a> <a href="/wiki/Chain_reaction" title="Chain reaction">chain reaction</a>, used in both <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a>. By measuring the mass of different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_nuclei" title="Atomic nuclei">atomic nuclei</a> and subtracting from that number the total mass of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protons" title="Protons">protons</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutrons" title="Neutrons">neutrons</a> as they would weigh separately, one gets the exact <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">binding energy</a> available in an <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">atomic nucleus</a>.
