This is used to calculate the energy released in any <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reaction" title="Nuclear reaction">nuclear reaction</a>, as the difference in the total mass of the nuclei that enter and exit the reaction.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Practical_examples">Practical examples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Practical examples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Einstein used the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centimeter_gram_second_system_of_units" title="Centimeter gram second system of units">CGS</a> system of units (centimeters, grams, seconds, dynes, and ergs), but the formula is independent of the system of units. In <a href="/wiki/Natural_units" title="Natural units">natural units</a>, the numerical value of the speed of light is set to equal 1, and the formula expresses an equality of numerical values: <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i> = <i>m</i></span>. In the <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">SI</a> system (expressing the ratio <span class="texhtml"><span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;"><i>E</i></span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;"><i>m</i></span></span></span> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joules" title="Joules">joules</a> per kilogram using the value of <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i></span> in <a href="/wiki/Metre_per_second" title="Metre per second">meters per second</a>):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="texhtml"><span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;"><i>E</i></span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;"><i>m</i></span></span> =</span> <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i><sup>2</sup> = (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7008299792458000000&#9824;</span>299<span style="margin-left:.25em;">792</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">458</span>&#160;m/s</span>)<sup>2</sup> =</span> <span class="texhtml"><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7016898755178736817&#9824;</span>89<span style="margin-left:.25em;">875</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">517</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">873</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">681</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">764</span>&#160;J/kg</span></span> (&#8776;&#160;9.0 &#215; 10<sup>16</sup> joules per kilogram).</dd></dl>\n<p>So the energy equivalent of one kilogram of mass is\n</p>\n<ul><li>89.9&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joules" title="Joules">petajoules</a></li>\n<li>25.0&#160;billion <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hour" title="Kilowatt-hour">kilowatt-hours</a> (&#8776;&#160;25,000&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GW%C2%B7h" title="GW&#183;h">GW&#183;h</a>)</li>\n<li>21.5&#160;trillion <a href="/wiki/Calorie" title="Calorie">kilocalories</a> (&#8776;&#160;21&#160;Pcal)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Conversion_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Conversion-34">[34]</a></sup></li>\n<li>85.2&#160;trillion <a href="/wiki/British_thermal_unit" title="British thermal unit">BTUs</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Conversion_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Conversion-34">[34]</a></sup></li>\n<li>0.0852 <a href="/wiki/Quad_(unit)" title="Quad (unit)">quads</a></li></ul>\n<p>or the energy released by combustion of the following:\n</p>\n<ul><li>21 500&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kiloton" title="Kiloton">kilotons</a> of <a href="/wiki/TNT_equivalent" title="TNT equivalent">TNT-equivalent</a> energy (&#8776;&#160;21&#160;Mt)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Conversion_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-Conversion-34">[34]</a></sup></li>\n<li><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7009263000000000000&#9824;</span>2<span style="margin-left:.25em;">630</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span></span> <a href="/wiki/Litre" title="Litre">litres</a> or <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7008695000000000000&#9824;</span>695<span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span></span> US <a href="/wiki/Gallon" title="Gallon">gallons</a> of automotive <a href="/wiki/Gasoline#Energy_content_(high_and_low_heating_value)" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a></li></ul>\n<p>Any time energy is generated, the process can be evaluated from an <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup></span> perspective. For instance, the "<a href="/wiki/Fat_Man" title="Fat Man">Gadget</a>"-style bomb used in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trinity_test" title="Trinity test">Trinity test</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Nagasaki" title="Bombing of Nagasaki">bombing of Nagasaki</a> had an explosive yield equivalent to 21&#160;kt of TNT. About 1&#160;kg of the approximately 6.15&#160;kg of plutonium in each of these bombs fissioned into lighter elements totaling almost exactly one gram less, after cooling. The electromagnetic radiation and kinetic energy (thermal and blast energy) released in this explosion carried the missing one gram of mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> This occurs because nuclear <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">binding energy</a> is released whenever elements with more than 62 nucleons fission.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>Another example is <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectric generation</a>. The electrical energy produced by <a href="/wiki/Grand_Coulee_Dam" title="Grand Coulee Dam">Grand Coulee Dam</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Water_turbine" title="Water turbine">turbines</a> every 3.7&#160;hours represents one gram of mass. This mass passes to electrical devices (such as lights in cities) powered by the generators, where it appears as a gram of heat and light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Turbine designers look at their equations in terms of pressure, torque, and RPM. However, Einstein\'s equations show that all energy has mass, and thus the electrical energy produced by a dam\'s generators, and the resulting heat and light, all retain their mass&#8212;which is equivalent to the energy. The potential energy&#8212;and equivalent mass&#8212;represented by the waters of the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_River" title="Columbia River">Columbia River</a> as it descends to the Pacific Ocean would be converted to heat due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viscous_friction" title="Viscous friction">viscous friction</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Turbulence" title="Turbulence">turbulence</a> of white water rapids and waterfalls were it not for the dam and its generators. This heat would remain as mass on site at the water, were it not for the equipment that converted some of this potential and kinetic energy into electrical energy, which can move from place to place (taking mass with it).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>Whenever energy is added to a system, the system gains mass, as shown when the equation is rearranged:\n</p>\n<ul><li>A spring\'s mass increases whenever it is put into compression or tension. Its added mass arises from the added potential energy stored within it, which is bound in the stretched chemical (electron) bonds linking the atoms within the spring.</li>\n<li>Raising the temperature of an object (increasing its heat energy) increases its mass. For example, consider the world\'s primary mass standard for the kilogram, made of platinum/iridium. If its temperature is allowed to change by 1&#160;&#176;C, its mass changes by 1.5 picograms (1&#160;pg = <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6985100000000000000&#9824;</span>1<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>&#8722;12</sup>&#160;g</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></li>\n<li>A spinning ball weighs more than a ball that is not spinning. Its increase of mass is exactly the equivalent of the mass of energy of rotation, which is itself the sum of the kinetic energies of all the moving parts of the ball. For example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Earth" title="The Earth">the Earth</a> itself is more massive due to its daily rotation, than it would be with no rotation. This <a href="/wiki/Rotational_energy" title="Rotational energy">rotational energy</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7029214000000000000&#9824;</span>2.14<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>29</sup>&#160;J</span>) represents 2.38 billion metric tons of added mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>Note that no net mass or energy is really created or lost in any of these examples and scenarios. Mass/energy simply moves from one place to another. These are some examples of the <i>transfer</i> of energy and mass in accordance with the <i>principle of mass&#8211;energy conservation</i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Efficiency">Efficiency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Efficiency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Although mass cannot be converted to energy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A._Wheeler,_1992._pp._248_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-A._Wheeler,_1992._pp._248-22">[22]</a></sup> in some reactions matter particles (which contain a form of rest energy) can be destroyed and the energy released can be converted to other types of energy that are more usable and obvious as forms of energy&#8212;such as light and energy of motion (heat, etc.). However, the total amount of energy and mass does not change in such a transformation. Even when particles are not destroyed, a certain fraction of the ill-defined "matter" in ordinary objects can be destroyed, and its associated energy liberated and made available as the more dramatic energies of light and heat, even though no identifiable real particles are destroyed, and even though (again) the total energy is unchanged (as also the total mass). Such conversions between types of energy (resting to active energy) happen in nuclear weapons, in which the protons and neutrons in atomic nuclei lose a small fraction of their average mass, but this mass loss is not due to the destruction of any protons or neutrons (or even, in general, lighter particles like electrons). Also the mass is not destroyed, but simply removed from the system in the form of heat and light from the reaction.\n</p><p>In nuclear reactions, typically only a small fraction of the total mass&#8211;energy of the bomb converts into the mass&#8211;energy of heat, light, radiation, and motion&#8212;which are "active" forms that can be used. When an atom fissions, it loses only about 0.1% of its mass (which escapes from the system and does not disappear), and additionally, in a bomb or reactor not all the atoms can fission. In a modern fission-based atomic bomb, the efficiency is only about 40%, so only 40% of the fissionable atoms actually fission, and only about 0.03% of the fissile core mass appears as energy in the end. In nuclear fusion, more of the mass is released as usable energy, roughly 0.3%. But in a fusion bomb, the bomb mass is partly casing and non-reacting components, so that in practicality, again (coincidentally) no more than about 0.03% of the total mass of the entire weapon is released as usable energy (which, again, retains the "missing" mass). See <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon_yield" title="Nuclear weapon yield">nuclear weapon yield</a> for practical details of this ratio in modern nuclear weapons.\n</p><p>In theory, it should be possible to destroy matter and convert all of the rest-energy associated with matter into heat and light (which would of course have the same mass), but none of the theoretically known methods are practical. One way to convert all the energy within matter into usable energy is to annihilate matter with <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">antimatter</a>. But <a href="/wiki/Baryon_asymmetry" title="Baryon asymmetry">antimatter is rare in our universe</a>, and must be made first. Due to inefficient mechanisms of production, making antimatter always requires far more usable energy than would be released when it was annihilated.\n</p><p>Since most of the mass of ordinary objects resides in protons and neutrons, converting all the energy of ordinary matter into more useful energy requires that the protons and neutrons be converted to lighter particles, or particles with no rest-mass at all. In the <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a> of particle physics, the <a href="/wiki/Baryon_number" title="Baryon number">number of protons plus neutrons</a> is nearly exactly conserved. Still, <a href="/wiki/Gerard_%27t_Hooft" title="Gerard \'t Hooft">Gerard \'t Hooft</a> showed that there is a process that converts protons and neutrons to antielectrons and neutrinos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> This is the weak SU(2) <a href="/wiki/Instanton" title="Instanton">instanton</a> proposed by Belavin Polyakov Schwarz and Tyupkin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> This process, can in principle destroy matter and convert all the energy of matter into neutrinos and usable energy, but it is normally extraordinarily slow. Later it became clear that this process happens at a fast rate at very high temperatures,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> since then, instanton-like configurations are copiously produced from <a href="/wiki/Statistical_mechanics" title="Statistical mechanics">thermal fluctuations</a>. The temperature required is so high that it would only have been reached shortly after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Big_bang" title="Big bang">big bang</a>.\n</p><p>Many extensions of the standard model contain <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_monopole" title="Magnetic monopole">magnetic monopoles</a>, and in some models of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_unification_theory" title="Grand unification theory">grand unification</a>, these monopoles catalyze <a href="/wiki/Proton_decay" title="Proton decay">proton decay</a>, a process known as the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Callan-Rubakov_effect&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Callan-Rubakov effect (page does not exist)">Callan-Rubakov effect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> This process would be an efficient mass&#8211;energy conversion at ordinary temperatures, but it requires making <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_monopole" title="Magnetic monopole">monopoles</a> and anti-monopoles first. The energy required to produce monopoles is believed to be enormous, but magnetic charge is conserved, so that the lightest monopole is stable. All these properties are deduced in theoretical models&#8212;magnetic monopoles have never been observed, nor have they been produced in any experiment so far.\n</p><p>A third known method of total matter&#8211;energy "conversion" (which again in practice only means conversion of one type of energy into a different type of energy), is using gravity, specifically black holes. <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hawking" title="Stephen Hawking">Stephen Hawking</a> theorized<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> that black holes radiate thermally with no regard to how they are formed. So, it is theoretically possible to throw matter into a black hole and use the emitted heat to generate power. According to the theory of <a href="/wiki/Hawking_radiation" title="Hawking radiation">Hawking radiation</a>, however, the black hole used radiates at a higher rate the smaller it is, producing usable powers at only small black hole masses, where usable may for example be something greater than the local background radiation. It is also worth noting that the ambient irradiated power would change with the mass of the black hole, increasing as the mass of the black hole decreases, or decreasing as the mass increases, at a rate where power is proportional to the inverse square of the mass. In a "practical" scenario, mass and energy could be dumped into the black hole to regulate this growth, or keep its size, and thus power output, near constant. This could result from the fact that mass and energy are lost from the hole with its thermal radiation.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="Mass.E2.80.93velocity_relationship"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mass&#8211;velocity_relationship">Mass&#8211;velocity relationship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Mass&#8211;velocity relationship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In developing <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special relativity</a>, Einstein found that the <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy#Relativistic_kinetic_energy_of_rigid_bodies" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> of a moving body is\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E_{k}=m_{0}(\\gamma -1)c^{2}={\\frac {m_{0}c^{2}}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}-m_{0}c^{2},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>k</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>&#947;<!-- &#947; --></mi>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mrow>\n<msqrt>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msup>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</msqrt>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>,</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E_{k}=m_{0}(\\gamma -1)c^{2}={\\frac {m_{0}c^{2}}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}-m_{0}c^{2},}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E_{k}=m_{0}(\\gamma -1)c^{2}={\\frac {m_{0}c^{2}}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}-m_{0}c^{2}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/58d5a0521ee4e6afd88f4e6d25717f2ca192b977" style="vertical-align: -4.671ex; width:39.502ex; height:8.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>with <span class="texhtml"><i>v</i></span> the <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocity</a>, <span class="texhtml"><i>m</i><sub>0</sub></span> the rest mass, and <span class="texhtml"><i>&#947;</i></span> the <a href="/wiki/Lorentz_factor" title="Lorentz factor">Lorentz factor</a>.\n</p><p>He included the second term on the right to make sure that for small velocities the energy would be the same as in classical mechanics, thus satisfying the <a href="/wiki/Correspondence_principle" title="Correspondence principle">correspondence principle</a>:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E_{k}={\\frac {1}{2}}m_{0}v^{2}+\\cdots }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>k</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msup>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mo>&#8943;<!-- &#8943; --></mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E_{k}={\\frac {1}{2}}m_{0}v^{2}+\\cdots }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E_{k}={\\frac {1}{2}}m_{0}v^{2}+\\cdots " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c0286a4511e3de685c17b22632881fd8873fd215" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:18.741ex; height:5.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>Without this second term, there would be an additional contribution in the energy when the particle is not moving.\n</p><p>Einstein found that the <a href="/wiki/Momentum#Modern_definitions_of_momentum" title="Momentum">total momentum</a> of a moving particle is:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle P={\\frac {m_{0}v}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>P</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mi>v</mi>\n</mrow>\n<msqrt>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msup>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</msqrt>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle P={\\frac {m_{0}v}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="P={\\frac {m_{0}v}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7764bc3287670153eb89bdaa916ac5fb01fdac7a" style="vertical-align: -4.671ex; width:15.119ex; height:7.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>It is this quantity that is conserved in collisions. The ratio of the momentum to the velocity is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relativistic_mass" title="Relativistic mass">relativistic mass</a>, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">m</span>.\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle m={\\frac {m_{0}}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msqrt>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msup>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</msqrt>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle m={\\frac {m_{0}}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="m={\\frac {m_{0}}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7ff2a40618bc9cd33363bf9282821e5aba01918e" style="vertical-align: -4.671ex; width:14.767ex; height:7.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>And the relativistic mass and the relativistic kinetic energy are related by the formula:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E_{k}=mc^{2}-m_{0}c^{2}.\\,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>k</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>.</mo>\n<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E_{k}=mc^{2}-m_{0}c^{2}.\\,}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E_{k}=mc^{2}-m_{0}c^{2}.\\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1c771de60025f50eaeb6e116fb741efd499c0cd2" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:19.034ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>Einstein wanted to omit the unnatural second term on the right-hand side, whose only purpose is to make the energy at rest zero, and to declare that the particle has a total energy, which obeys:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E=mc^{2}\\,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E=mc^{2}\\,}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E=mc^{2}\\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a22aba8c4231afd4e22ed2359575f14c6a407520" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:9.363ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>which is a sum of the rest energy <span class="texhtml"><i>m</i><sub>0</sub><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span> and the kinetic energy. This total energy is mathematically more elegant, and fits better with the momentum in relativity. But to come to this conclusion, Einstein needed to think carefully about collisions. This expression for the energy implied that matter at rest has a huge amount of energy, and it is not clear whether this energy is physically real, or just a mathematical artifact with no physical meaning.\n</p><p>In a collision process where all the rest-masses are the same at the beginning as at the end, either expression for the energy is conserved. The two expressions only differ by a constant that is the same at the beginning and at the end of the collision. Still, by analyzing the situation where particles are thrown off a heavy central particle, it is easy to see that the inertia of the central particle is reduced by the total energy emitted. This allowed Einstein to conclude that the inertia of a heavy particle is increased or diminished according to the energy it absorbs or emits.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relativistic_mass">Relativistic mass</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Relativistic mass">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mass_in_special_relativity" title="Mass in special relativity">Mass in special relativity</a></div>\n<p>After Einstein first made his proposal, it became clear that the word mass can have two different meanings. Some denote the <i>relativistic mass</i> with an explicit index:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle m_{\\mathrm {rel} }={\\frac {m_{0}}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="normal">r</mi>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">l</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msqrt>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msup>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</msqrt>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle m_{\\mathrm {rel} }={\\frac {m_{0}}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="m_{\\mathrm {rel} }={\\frac {m_{0}}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c09350da6ce711e11f8ddcef602aff5bd9b84d76" style="vertical-align: -4.671ex; width:17.478ex; height:7.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>This mass is the ratio of momentum to velocity, and it is also the relativistic energy divided by <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span> (it is not Lorentz-invariant, in contrast to <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle m_{0}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle m_{0}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="m_{0}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3a6ff51ee949104fe6fae553cfbdfba29d5fac1e" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:3.095ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span>). The equation <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i> = <i>m</i><sub>rel</sub><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span> holds for moving objects. When the velocity is small, the relativistic mass and the rest mass are almost exactly the same.\n</p>\n<ul><li><span class="texhtml"><i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup></span> either means <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i> = <i>m</i><sub>0</sub><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span> for an object at rest, or <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i> = <i>m</i><sub>rel</sub><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span> when the object is moving.</li></ul>\n<p>Also Einstein (following <a href="/wiki/Hendrik_Lorentz" title="Hendrik Lorentz">Hendrik Lorentz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Max_Abraham" title="Max Abraham">Max Abraham</a>) used velocity- and direction-dependent mass concepts (<a href="/wiki/Mass_in_special_relativity#Early_developments:_transverse_and_longitudinal_mass" title="Mass in special relativity">longitudinal and transverse mass</a>) in his 1905 electrodynamics paper and in another paper in 1906.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>\nHowever, in his first paper on <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup></span> (1905), he treated <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">m</span> as what would now be called the <i>rest mass</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inertia_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-inertia-3">[3]</a></sup> Some claim that (in later years) he did not like the idea of "relativistic mass".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Okun_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Okun-46">[46]</a></sup>&#160; When modern physicists say "mass", they are usually talking about rest mass, since if they meant "relativistic mass", they would just say "energy".\n</p><p>Considerable debate has ensued over the use of the concept "relativistic mass" and the connection of "mass" in relativity to "mass" in Newtonian dynamics. For example, one view is that only rest mass is a viable concept and is a property of the particle; while relativistic mass is a conglomeration of particle properties and properties of spacetime. A perspective that avoids this debate, due to Kjell V&#248;yenli, is that the Newtonian concept of mass as a particle property and the relativistic concept of mass have to be viewed as embedded in their own theories and as having no precise connection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jammer_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jammer-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-V&#248;yenli_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-V&#248;yenli-48">[48]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Low_speed_expansion">Low speed expansion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Low speed expansion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>We can rewrite the expression <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i> = <i>&#947;m</i><sub>0</sub><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span> as a <a href="/wiki/Taylor_series" title="Taylor series">Taylor series</a>:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E=m_{0}c^{2}\\left[1+{\\frac {1}{2}}\\left({\\frac {v}{c}}\\right)^{2}+{\\frac {3}{8}}\\left({\\frac {v}{c}}\\right)^{4}+{\\frac {5}{16}}\\left({\\frac {v}{c}}\\right)^{6}+\\ldots \\right].}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mrow>\n<mo>[</mo>\n<mrow>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<msup>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mi>c</mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>3</mn>\n<mn>8</mn>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<msup>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mi>c</mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>4</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>5</mn>\n<mn>16</mn>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<msup>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mi>c</mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>6</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mo>&#8230;<!-- &#8230; --></mo>\n</mrow>\n<mo>]</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E=m_{0}c^{2}\\left[1+{\\frac {1}{2}}\\left({\\frac {v}{c}}\\right)^{2}+{\\frac {3}{8}}\\left({\\frac {v}{c}}\\right)^{4}+{\\frac {5}{16}}\\left({\\frac {v}{c}}\\right)^{6}+\\ldots \\right].}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E=m_{0}c^{2}\\left[1+{\\frac {1}{2}}\\left({\\frac {v}{c}}\\right)^{2}+{\\frac {3}{8}}\\left({\\frac {v}{c}}\\right)^{4}+{\\frac {5}{16}}\\left({\\frac {v}{c}}\\right)^{6}+\\ldots \\right]." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81c7043d5fccc00eff27036f20854c4c72874189" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:53.692ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>For speeds much smaller than the speed of light, higher-order terms in this expression get smaller and smaller because <span class="texhtml"><span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;"><i>v</i></span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;"><i>c</i></span></span></span> is small. For low speeds we can ignore all but the first two terms:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E\\approx m_{0}c^{2}+{\\frac {1}{2}}m_{0}v^{2}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mo>&#8776;<!-- &#8776; --></mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msup>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E\\approx m_{0}c^{2}+{\\frac {1}{2}}m_{0}v^{2}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E\\approx m_{0}c^{2}+{\\frac {1}{2}}m_{0}v^{2}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/54b78d93a8606c34816a3605a0c38162bba6a4f3" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:20.792ex; height:5.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>The total energy is a sum of the rest energy and the <a href="/wiki/Classical_mechanics" title="Classical mechanics">Newtonian</a> <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a>.\n</p><p>The classical energy equation ignores both the <span class="texhtml"><i>m</i><sub>0</sub><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span> part, and the high-speed corrections. This is appropriate, because all the high-order corrections are small. Since only <i>changes</i> in energy affect the behavior of objects, whether we include the <span class="texhtml"><i>m</i><sub>0</sub><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span> part makes no difference, since it is constant. For the same reason, it is possible to subtract the rest energy from the total energy in relativity. By considering the emission of energy in different frames, Einstein could show that the rest energy has a real physical meaning.\n</p><p>The higher-order terms are extra corrections to Newtonian mechanics, and become important at higher speeds. The Newtonian equation is only a low-speed approximation, but an extraordinarily good one. All of the calculations used in putting astronauts on the moon, for example, could have been done using Newton\'s equations without any of the higher-order corrections.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\nThe total mass energy equivalence should also include the rotational and vibrational kinetic energies as well as the linear kinetic energy at low speeds.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>While Einstein was the first to have correctly deduced the mass&#8211;energy equivalence formula, he was not the first to have related energy with mass. But nearly all previous authors thought that the energy that contributes to mass comes only from electromagnetic fields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jann_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-jann-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whit_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-whit-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mill_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-mill-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-darr_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-darr-52">[52]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Newton:_matter_and_light">Newton: matter and light</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Newton: matter and light">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1717 <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> speculated that light particles and matter particles were interconvertible in "Query 30" of the <i><a href="/wiki/Opticks" title="Opticks">Opticks</a></i>, where he asks:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Are not the gross bodies and light convertible into one another, and may not bodies receive much of their activity from the particles of light which enter their composition?\n</p></blockquote>\n<h3><span id="Swedenborg:_matter_composed_of_.22pure_and_total_motion.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id=\'Swedenborg:_matter_composed_of_"pure_and_total_motion"\'>Swedenborg: matter composed of "pure and total motion"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title=\'Edit section: Swedenborg: matter composed of "pure and total motion"\'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1734 the Swedish scientist and theologian <a href="/wiki/Emanuel_Swedenborg" title="Emanuel Swedenborg">Emanuel Swedenborg</a> in his <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=The_Principia_(book)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="The Principia (book) (page does not exist)">Principia</a></i> theorized that all matter is ultimately composed of dimensionless points of "pure and total motion."  He described this motion as being without force, direction or speed, but having the potential for force, direction and speed everywhere within it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electromagnetic_mass">Electromagnetic mass</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Electromagnetic mass">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_mass" title="Electromagnetic mass">Electromagnetic mass</a></div>\n<p>There were many attempts in the 19th and the beginning of the 20th century&#8212;like those of <a href="/wiki/J._J._Thomson" title="J. J. Thomson">J. J. Thomson</a> (1881), <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Heaviside" title="Oliver Heaviside">Oliver Heaviside</a> (1888), and <a href="/wiki/George_Frederick_Charles_Searle" title="George Frederick Charles Searle">George Frederick Charles Searle</a> (1897), <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Wien" title="Wilhelm Wien">Wilhelm Wien</a> (1900), <a href="/wiki/Max_Abraham" title="Max Abraham">Max Abraham</a> (1902), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hendrik_Antoon_Lorentz" title="Hendrik Antoon Lorentz">Hendrik Antoon Lorentz</a> (1904) &#8212; to understand how the mass of a charged object depends on the electrostatic field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jann_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-jann-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whit_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-whit-50">[50]</a></sup> This concept was called <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_mass" title="Electromagnetic mass">electromagnetic mass</a>, and was considered as being dependent on velocity and direction as well. Lorentz (1904) gave the following expressions for longitudinal and transverse electromagnetic mass:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle m_{L}={\\frac {m_{0}}{\\left({\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}\\right)^{3}}},\\quad m_{T}={\\frac {m_{0}}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>L</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msup>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msqrt>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msup>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</msqrt>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>3</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>,</mo>\n<mspace width="1em"></mspace>\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>T</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msqrt>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msup>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</msqrt>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle m_{L}={\\frac {m_{0}}{\\left({\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}\\right)^{3}}},\\quad m_{T}={\\frac {m_{0}}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="m_{L}={\\frac {m_{0}}{\\left({\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}\\right)^{3}}},\\quad m_{T}={\\frac {m_{0}}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/576dbb2e5906a91ffd4e0586b9131deac651a40e" style="vertical-align: -5.171ex; width:39.46ex; height:8.009ex;"/></span>,</dd></dl>\n<p>where\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle m_{0}={\\frac {4}{3}}{\\frac {E_{em}}{c^{2}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>4</mn>\n<mn>3</mn>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msub>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mi>m</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle m_{0}={\\frac {4}{3}}{\\frac {E_{em}}{c^{2}}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="m_{0}={\\frac {4}{3}}{\\frac {E_{em}}{c^{2}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/707c96a55ca5086e56851a29ffdecd7b369f1ad6" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:13.184ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiation_pressure_and_inertia">Radiation pressure and inertia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Radiation pressure and inertia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_mass#Inertia_of_energy_and_radiation_paradoxes" title="Electromagnetic mass">Electromagnetic mass &#167;&#160;Inertia of energy and radiation paradoxes</a></div>\n<p>Another way of deriving some sort of electromagnetic mass was based on the concept of <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pressure" title="Radiation pressure">radiation pressure</a>. In 1900, <a href="/wiki/Henri_Poincar%C3%A9" title="Henri Poincar&#233;">Henri Poincar&#233;</a> associated electromagnetic radiation energy with a "fictitious fluid" having momentum and mass<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-action_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-action-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle m_{em}={\\frac {E_{em}}{c^{2}}}\\,.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mi>m</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msub>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mi>m</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle m_{em}={\\frac {E_{em}}{c^{2}}}\\,.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle m_{em}={\\frac {E_{em}}{c^{2}}}\\,.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2a82f867b458ee05a84ba71d142ccfe8c220bc8e" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:13.607ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>By that, Poincar&#233; tried to save the <a href="/wiki/Center_of_mass" title="Center of mass">center of mass</a> theorem in Lorentz\'s theory, though his treatment led to radiation paradoxes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-darr_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-darr-52">[52]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hasen%C3%B6hrl" title="Friedrich Hasen&#246;hrl">Friedrich Hasen&#246;hrl</a> showed in 1904, that electromagnetic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cavity_radiation" title="Cavity radiation">cavity radiation</a> contributes the "apparent mass"\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle m_{0}={\\frac {4}{3}}{\\frac {E_{em}}{c^{2}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>4</mn>\n<mn>3</mn>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msub>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mi>m</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle m_{0}={\\frac {4}{3}}{\\frac {E_{em}}{c^{2}}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="m_{0}={\\frac {4}{3}}{\\frac {E_{em}}{c^{2}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/707c96a55ca5086e56851a29ffdecd7b369f1ad6" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:13.184ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>to the cavity\'s mass. He argued that this implies mass dependence on temperature as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Einstein:_mass.E2.80.93energy_equivalence"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Einstein:_mass&#8211;energy_equivalence">Einstein: mass&#8211;energy equivalence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Einstein: mass&#8211;energy equivalence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> did not formulate exactly the formula <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup></span> in his 1905 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annus_Mirabilis_Papers" title="Annus Mirabilis Papers"><i>Annus Mirabilis</i> paper</a> "Does the Inertia of an object Depend Upon Its Energy Content?";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inertia_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-inertia-3">[3]</a></sup> rather, the paper states that if a body gives off the energy <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">L</span> in the form of radiation, its mass diminishes by <span class="texhtml"><span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;"><i>L</i></span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;"><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span></span></span>. (Here, "radiation" means <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a>, or light, and mass means the ordinary Newtonian mass of a slow-moving object.) This formulation relates only a change <span class="texhtml">&#916;<i>m</i></span> in mass to a change <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">L</span> in energy without requiring the absolute relationship.\n</p><p>Objects with zero mass presumably have zero energy, so the extension that all mass is proportional to energy is obvious from this result. In 1905, even the hypothesis that changes in energy are accompanied by changes in mass was untested. Not until the discovery of the first type of antimatter (the <a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">positron</a> in 1932) was it found that all of the mass of pairs of resting particles could be converted to radiation.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="The_first_derivation_by_Einstein_.281905.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_first_derivation_by_Einstein_(1905)">The first derivation by Einstein (1905)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: The first derivation by Einstein (1905)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Already in his relativity paper "On the electrodynamics of moving bodies", Einstein derived the correct expression for the kinetic energy of particles:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E_{k}=mc^{2}\\left({\\frac {1}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}-1\\right)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>k</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<msqrt>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msup>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</msqrt>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E_{k}=mc^{2}\\left({\\frac {1}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}-1\\right)}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E_{k}=mc^{2}\\left({\\frac {1}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}-1\\right)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b76fdf9c519a9d2315e21310c49ae6d46749c44d" style="vertical-align: -4.671ex; width:28.088ex; height:10.509ex;"/></span>.</dd></dl>\n<p>Now the question remained open as to which formulation applies to bodies at rest. This was tackled by Einstein in his paper "Does the inertia of a body depend upon its energy content?", where he used a body emitting two light pulses in opposite directions, having energies of <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i><sub>0</sub></span> before and <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i><sub>1</sub></span> after the emission as seen in its rest frame. As seen from a moving frame, this becomes <span class="texhtml"><i>H</i><sub>0</sub></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>H</i><sub>1</sub></span>. Einstein obtained:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\left(H_{0}-E_{0}\\right)-\\left(H_{1}-E_{1}\\right)=E\\left({\\frac {1}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}-1\\right)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi>H</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi>H</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<msqrt>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msup>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</msqrt>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\left(H_{0}-E_{0}\\right)-\\left(H_{1}-E_{1}\\right)=E\\left({\\frac {1}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}-1\\right)}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\left(H_{0}-E_{0}\\right)-\\left(H_{1}-E_{1}\\right)=E\\left({\\frac {1}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}-1\\right)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d41b114ac1ac4160f8362a68a47e421a6ccb0ed3" style="vertical-align: -4.671ex; width:46.608ex; height:10.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>then he argued that <span class="texhtml"><i>H</i> &#8722; <i>E</i></span> can only differ from the kinetic energy <span class="texhtml"><i>K</i></span> by an additive constant, which gives\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle K_{0}-K_{1}=E\\left({\\frac {1}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}-1\\right)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>K</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>K</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<msqrt>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msup>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</msqrt>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle K_{0}-K_{1}=E\\left({\\frac {1}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}-1\\right)}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="K_{0}-K_{1}=E\\left({\\frac {1}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}-1\\right)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87bcf920994e80d8cb8894f10e5c5703d0ede7d4" style="vertical-align: -4.671ex; width:31.854ex; height:10.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>Neglecting effects higher than third order in <span class="texhtml"><span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;"><i>v</i></span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;"><i>c</i></span></span></span> after a <a href="/wiki/Taylor_series" title="Taylor series">Taylor series</a> expansion of the right side of this gives:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle K_{0}-K_{1}={\\frac {E}{c^{2}}}{\\frac {v^{2}}{2}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>K</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>K</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msup>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle K_{0}-K_{1}={\\frac {E}{c^{2}}}{\\frac {v^{2}}{2}}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="K_{0}-K_{1}={\\frac {E}{c^{2}}}{\\frac {v^{2}}{2}}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/527d931f674c7fc58c88f383f3dd25b3720bd7db" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:18.555ex; height:6.009ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>Einstein concluded that the emission reduces the body\'s mass by <span class="texhtml"><span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;"><i>E</i></span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;"><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span></span></span>, and that the mass of a body is a measure of its energy content.\n</p><p>The correctness of Einstein\'s 1905 derivation of <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup></span> was criticized by <a href="/wiki/Max_Planck" title="Max Planck">Max Planck</a> (1907), who argued that it is only valid to first approximation. Another criticism was formulated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herbert_Ives" title="Herbert Ives">Herbert Ives</a> (1952) and <a href="/wiki/Max_Jammer" title="Max Jammer">Max Jammer</a> (1961), asserting that Einstein\'s derivation is based on <a href="/wiki/Begging_the_question" title="Begging the question">begging the question</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jammer2_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-jammer2-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>\nOn the other hand, <a href="/wiki/John_Stachel" title="John Stachel">John Stachel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roberto_Torretti" title="Roberto Torretti">Roberto Torretti</a> (1982) argued that Ives\' criticism was wrong, and that Einstein\'s derivation was correct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>\nHans Ohanian (2008) agreed with Stachel/Torretti\'s criticism of Ives, though he argued that Einstein\'s derivation was wrong for other reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> For a recent review, see Hecht (2011).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hecht_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-hecht-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_version">Alternative version</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Alternative version">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>An alternative version of Einstein\'s <a href="/wiki/Thought_experiment" title="Thought experiment">thought experiment</a> was proposed by <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Rohrlich" title="Fritz Rohrlich">Fritz Rohrlich</a> (1990), who based his reasoning on the <a href="/wiki/Doppler_effect" title="Doppler effect">Doppler effect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>\nLike Einstein, he considered a body at rest with mass <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">M</span>. If the body is examined in a frame moving with nonrelativistic velocity <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">v</span>, it is no longer at rest and in the moving frame it has momentum <span class="texhtml"><i>P</i> = <i>Mv</i></span>. Then he supposed the body emits two pulses of light to the left and to the right, each carrying an equal amount of energy <span class="texhtml"><span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;"><i>E</i></span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">2</span></span></span>. In its rest frame, the object remains at rest after the emission since the two beams are equal in strength and carry opposite momentum.\n</p><p>However, if the same process is considered in a frame that moves with velocity <span class="texhtml"><i>v</i></span> to the left, the pulse moving to the left is <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshifted</a>, while the pulse moving to the right is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_shift" title="Blue shift">blue shifted</a>. The blue light carries more momentum than the red light, so that the momentum of the light in the moving frame is not balanced: the light is carrying some net momentum to the right.\n</p><p>The object has not changed its velocity before or after the emission. Yet in this frame it has lost some right-momentum to the light. The only way it could have lost momentum is by losing mass. This also solves Poincar&#233;\'s radiation paradox, discussed above.\n</p><p>The velocity is small, so the right-moving light is blueshifted by an amount equal to the nonrelativistic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doppler_shift" title="Doppler shift">Doppler shift</a> factor <span class="texhtml">1 &#8722; <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;"><i>v</i></span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;"><i>c</i></span></span></span>. The momentum of the light is its energy divided by <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">c</span>, and it is increased by a factor of <span class="texhtml"><span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;"><i>v</i></span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;"><i>c</i></span></span></span>. So the right-moving light is carrying an extra momentum <span class="texhtml">&#916;<i>P</i></span> given by:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\Delta P={v \\over c}{E \\over 2c}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mi>P</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mi>c</mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow>\n<mn>2</mn>\n<mi>c</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\Delta P={v \\over c}{E \\over 2c}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\Delta P={v \\over c}{E \\over 2c}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4ec943bffa94c4b011a932a8f1703ee630e5df1c" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:12.396ex; height:5.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>The left-moving light carries a little less momentum, by the same amount <span class="texhtml">&#916;<i>P</i></span>. So the total right-momentum in the light is twice <span class="texhtml">&#916;<i>P</i></span>. This is the right-momentum that the object lost.\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle 2\\Delta P=v{E \\over c^{2}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mn>2</mn>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mi>P</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle 2\\Delta P=v{E \\over c^{2}}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="2\\Delta P=v{E \\over c^{2}}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/07b5882ed796cfb320c6f8fb4987f1121c97f94b" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:12.614ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>The momentum of the object in the moving frame after the emission is reduced to this amount:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle P\'=Mv-2\\Delta P=\\left(M-{E \\over c^{2}}\\right)v.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msup>\n<mi>P</mi>\n<mo>&#8242;</mo>\n</msup>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>M</mi>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mn>2</mn>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mi>P</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow>\n<mi>M</mi>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle P\'=Mv-2\\Delta P=\\left(M-{E \\over c^{2}}\\right)v.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="P\'=Mv-2\\Delta P=\\left(M-{E \\over c^{2}}\\right)v." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0d90ff6cae787518ff822cfcc6639c302c5514af" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:33.72ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>So the change in the object\'s mass is equal to the total energy lost divided by <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span>. Since any emission of energy can be carried out by a two step process, where first the energy is emitted as light and then the light is converted to some other form of energy, any emission of energy is accompanied by a loss of mass. Similarly, by considering absorption, a gain in energy is accompanied by a gain in mass.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Relativistic_center-of-mass_theorem_.281906.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Relativistic_center-of-mass_theorem_(1906)">Relativistic center-of-mass theorem (1906)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Relativistic center-of-mass theorem (1906)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Like Poincar&#233;, Einstein concluded in 1906 that the inertia of electromagnetic energy is a necessary condition for the center-of-mass theorem to hold. On this occasion, Einstein referred to Poincar&#233;\'s 1900 paper and wrote:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Although the merely formal considerations, which we will need for the proof, are already mostly contained in a work by H. Poincar&#233;<sup>2</sup>, for the sake of clarity I will not rely on that work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>In Einstein\'s more physical, as opposed to formal or mathematical, point of view, there was no need for fictitious masses. He could avoid the <i><a href="/wiki/Perpetual_motion" title="Perpetual motion">perpetuum mobile</a></i> problem because, on the basis of the mass&#8211;energy equivalence, he could show that the transport of inertia that accompanies the emission and absorption of radiation solves the problem. Poincar&#233;\'s rejection of the principle of action&#8211;reaction can be avoided through Einstein\'s <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup></span>, because mass conservation appears as a special case of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_conservation_law" title="Energy conservation law">energy conservation law</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Others">Others</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Others">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>During the nineteenth century there were several speculative attempts to show that mass and energy were proportional in various <a href="/wiki/Aether_theories" title="Aether theories">ether theories</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> In 1873 <a href="/wiki/Nikolay_Umov" title="Nikolay Umov">Nikolay Umov</a> pointed out a relation between mass and energy for ether in the form of <span class="texhtml"><i>&#1045;</i> = <i>kmc</i><sup>2</sup></span>, where <span class="texhtml">0.5 &#8804; <i>k</i> &#8804; 1</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The writings of <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Tolver_Preston" title="Samuel Tolver Preston">Samuel Tolver Preston</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> and a 1903 paper by <a href="/wiki/Olinto_De_Pretto" title="Olinto De Pretto">Olinto De Pretto</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-math_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-math-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> presented a mass&#8211;energy relation.
