Bartocci (1999) observed that there were only <a href="/wiki/Six_degrees_of_separation" title="Six degrees of separation">three degrees of separation</a> linking De Pretto to Einstein, concluding that Einstein was probably aware of De Pretto\'s work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Preston and De Pretto, following <a href="/wiki/Le_Sage%27s_theory_of_gravitation" title="Le Sage\'s theory of gravitation">Le Sage</a>, imagined that the universe was filled with an ether of tiny particles that always move at speed <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">c</span>. Each of these particles has a kinetic energy of <span class="texhtml"><i>mc</i><sup>2</sup></span> up to a small numerical factor. The nonrelativistic kinetic energy formula did not always include the traditional factor of <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">1</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">2</span></span>, since <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gottfried_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Leibniz">Leibniz</a> introduced kinetic energy without it, and the <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">1</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">2</span></span> is largely conventional in prerelativistic physics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> By assuming that every particle has a mass that is the sum of the masses of the ether particles, the authors concluded that all matter contains an amount of kinetic energy either given by <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup></span> or <span class="texhtml">2<i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup></span> depending on the convention. A particle ether was usually considered unacceptably speculative science at the time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> and since these authors did not formulate relativity, their reasoning is completely different from that of Einstein, who used relativity to change frames.\n</p><p>Independently, <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Le_Bon" title="Gustave Le Bon">Gustave Le Bon</a> in 1905 speculated that atoms could release large amounts of latent energy, reasoning from an all-encompassing qualitative philosophy of physics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radioactivity_and_nuclear_energy">Radioactivity and nuclear energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Radioactivity and nuclear energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>It was quickly noted after the discovery of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactivity" title="Radioactivity">radioactivity</a> in 1897, that the total energy due to radioactive processes is about one <i>million times</i> greater than that involved in any known molecular change. However, it raised the question where this energy is coming from. After eliminating the idea of absorption and emission of some sort of <a href="/wiki/Le_Sage%27s_theory_of_gravitation" title="Le Sage\'s theory of gravitation">Lesagian ether particles</a>, the existence of a huge amount of latent energy, stored within matter, was proposed by <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Soddy" title="Frederick Soddy">Frederick Soddy</a> in 1903. Rutherford also suggested that this internal energy is stored within normal matter as well. He went on to speculate in 1904:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>If it were ever found possible to control at will the rate of disintegration of the radio-elements, an enormous amount of energy could be obtained from a small quantity of matter.\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Einstein\'s equation is in no way an explanation of the large energies released in radioactive decay (this comes from the powerful <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_force" title="Nuclear force">nuclear forces</a> involved; forces that were still unknown in 1905). In any case, the enormous energy released from radioactive decay (which had been measured by Rutherford) was much more easily measured than the (still small) change in the gross mass of materials as a result. Einstein\'s equation, by theory, can give these energies by measuring mass differences before and after reactions, but in practice, these mass differences in 1905 were still too small to be measured in bulk. Prior to this, the ease of measuring radioactive decay energies with a calorimeter was thought possibly likely to allow measurement of changes in mass difference, as a check on Einstein\'s equation itself. Einstein mentions in his 1905 paper that mass&#8211;energy equivalence might perhaps be tested with radioactive decay, which releases enough energy (the quantitative amount known roughly by 1905) to possibly be "weighed," when missing from the system (having been given off as heat). However, radioactivity seemed to proceed at its own unalterable (and quite slow, for radioactives known then) pace, and even when simple nuclear reactions became possible using proton bombardment, the idea that these great amounts of usable energy could be liberated at will with any practicality, proved difficult to substantiate. Rutherford was reported in 1933 to have declared that this energy could not be exploited efficiently: "Anyone who expects a source of power from the transformation of the atom is talking <a href="/wiki/Moonshine" title="Moonshine">moonshine</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Einstein_-_Time_Magazine_-_July_1,_1946.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="362" data-file-width="275" decoding="async" height="290" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/57/Einstein_-_Time_Magazine_-_July_1%2C_1946.jpg/220px-Einstein_-_Time_Magazine_-_July_1%2C_1946.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/5/57/Einstein_-_Time_Magazine_-_July_1%2C_1946.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Einstein_-_Time_Magazine_-_July_1,_1946.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The popular connection between Einstein, <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup></span>, and the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">atomic bomb</a> was prominently indicated on the cover of <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine in July 1946 by the writing of the equation on the <a href="/wiki/Mushroom_cloud" title="Mushroom cloud">mushroom cloud</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>This situation changed dramatically in 1932 with the discovery of the neutron and its mass, allowing mass differences for single <a href="/wiki/Nuclide" title="Nuclide">nuclides</a> and their reactions to be calculated directly, and compared with the sum of masses for the particles that made up their composition. In 1933, the energy released from the reaction of lithium-7 plus protons giving rise to 2 alpha particles (as noted above by Rutherford), allowed Einstein\'s equation to be tested to an error of &#177;0.5%. However, scientists still did not see such reactions as a practical source of power, due to the energy cost of accelerating reaction particles.\n</p><p>After the very public demonstration of huge energies released from <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki</a> in 1945, the equation <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup></span> became directly linked in the public eye with the power and peril of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapons</a>. The equation was featured as early as page 2 of the <a href="/wiki/Smyth_Report" title="Smyth Report">Smyth Report</a>, the official 1945 release by the US government on the development of the atomic bomb, and by 1946 the equation was linked closely enough with Einstein\'s work that the cover of <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine prominently featured a picture of Einstein next to an image of a <a href="/wiki/Mushroom_cloud" title="Mushroom cloud">mushroom cloud</a> emblazoned with the equation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Einstein himself had only a minor role in the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a>: he had <a href="/wiki/Einstein%E2%80%93Szil%C3%A1rd_letter" title="Einstein&#8211;Szil&#225;rd letter">cosigned a letter</a> to the U.S. President in 1939 urging funding for research into atomic energy, warning that an atomic bomb was theoretically possible. The letter persuaded Roosevelt to devote a significant portion of the wartime budget to atomic research. Without a security clearance, Einstein\'s only scientific contribution was an analysis of an <a href="/wiki/Isotope_separation" title="Isotope separation">isotope separation</a> method in theoretical terms. It was inconsequential, on account of Einstein not being given sufficient information (for security reasons) to fully work on the problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup></span> is useful for understanding the amount of energy potentially released in a fission reaction, it was not strictly necessary to develop the weapon, once the fission process was known, and its energy measured at 200&#160;MeV (which was directly possible, using a quantitative Geiger counter, at that time). As the physicist and Manhattan Project participant <a href="/wiki/Robert_Serber" title="Robert Serber">Robert Serber</a> put it: "Somehow the popular notion took hold long ago that Einstein\'s theory of relativity, in particular his famous equation <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup></span>, plays some essential role in the theory of fission. Albert Einstein had a part in alerting the United States government to the possibility of building an atomic bomb, but his theory of relativity is not required in discussing fission. The theory of fission is what physicists call a non-relativistic theory, meaning that relativistic effects are too small to affect the dynamics of the fission process significantly."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> However the association between <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup></span> and nuclear energy has since stuck, and because of this association, and its simple expression of the ideas of Albert Einstein himself, it has become "the world\'s most famous equation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-famous_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-famous-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While Serber\'s view of the strict lack of need to use mass&#8211;energy equivalence in designing the atomic bomb is correct, it does not take into account the pivotal role this relationship played in making the fundamental leap to the initial hypothesis that large atoms were energetically <i>allowed</i> to split into approximately equal parts (before this energy was in fact measured). In late 1938, <a href="/wiki/Lise_Meitner" title="Lise Meitner">Lise Meitner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Otto_Robert_Frisch" title="Otto Robert Frisch">Otto Robert Frisch</a>&#8212;while on a winter walk during which they solved the meaning of Hahn\'s experimental results and introduced the idea that would be called atomic fission&#8212;directly used Einstein\'s equation to help them understand the quantitative energetics of the reaction that overcame the "surface tension-like" forces that hold the nucleus together, and allowed the fission fragments to separate to a configuration from which their charges could force them into an energetic <i>fission</i>. To do this, they used <i>packing fraction</i>, or nuclear <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">binding energy</a> values for elements, which Meitner had memorized. These, together with use of <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup></span> allowed them to realize on the spot that the basic fission process was energetically possible:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p> ...We walked up and down in the snow, I on skis and she on foot. ...and gradually the idea took shape... explained by Bohr\'s idea that the nucleus is like a liquid drop; such a drop might elongate and divide itself... We knew there were strong forces that would resist, ..just as surface tension. But nuclei differed from ordinary drops. At this point we both sat down on a tree trunk and started to calculate on scraps of paper. ...the Uranium nucleus might indeed be a very wobbly, unstable drop, ready to divide itself... But, ...when the two drops separated they would be driven apart by electrical repulsion, about 200 MeV in all. Fortunately Lise Meitner remembered how to compute the masses of nuclei... and worked out that the two nuclei formed... would be lighter by about one-fifth the mass of a proton. Now whenever mass disappears energy is created, according to Einstein\'s formula <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup></span>, and... the mass was just equivalent to 200 MeV; it all fitted!<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div energy</a></li></ul></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\tilde {v}}=dx^{(4)}/d\\tau }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\tilde {v}}=dx^{(4)}/d\\tau " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9035c817aed7419b93fa32ee11a54fe6d64f6066" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; --></mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>d</mi>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mo>&#8901;<!-- &#8901; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msqrt>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<msup>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</msqrt>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle d\\tau =dt\\cdot {\\sqrt {1-(v^{2}/c^{2})}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="d\\tau =dt\\cdot {\\sqrt {1-(v^{2}/c^{2})}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7cf706542b469daab2fc6d53c76ec92b2e989341" style="vertical-align: -1.671ex; However, the energy&#8211;momentum four-vector <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle p^{(4)}=m_{0}\\cdot dx^{(4)}/d\\tau }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msup>\n<mi>p</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mn>4</mn>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>&#8901;<!-- &#8901; --></mo>\n<mi>d</mi>\n<msup>\n<mi>x</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mn>4</mn>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mi>d</mi>\n<mi>&#964;<!-- &#964; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle p^{(4)}=m_{0}\\cdot dx^{(4)}/d\\tau }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="p^{(4)}=m_{0}\\cdot dx^{(4)}/d\\tau " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0714640d614b798eda55fccfe0f79fcd22a392f3" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; margin-left: -0.089ex; width:19.924ex; height:3.343ex;"/></span> is a genuine Minkowski four-vector, and the intrinsic origin of the square root in the definition of the relativistic mass is the distinction between <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">d&#964;</span> and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">dt</span>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-tipler-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-tipler_24-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation" id="CITEREFPaul_Allen_TiplerRalph_A._Llewellyn2003">Paul Allen Tipler; Ralph A.
